More specifically, Apollo 6 unexpectedly experienced pogo at 5 Hz between 105-140 seconds during first stage (S-IC) boost with 0.60 g maximum acceleration at the command module and 0.33 g at the aft of the vehicle.\n\nThe thrust variations caused the Saturn V to experience a g-force of ±0.6 g (5.9 m/s2), though it had only been designed for a maximum of 0.25 g (2.5 m/s2).\n\nDuring the last ten seconds of that first stage burn, the rocket experienced longitudinal oscillations called \"pogo effect.\" Pogo occurred when a partial vacuum in the fuel and oxidizer feed lines reached the engine firing chamber causing the engine to skip.\n\n## Mass\n\nThe spacecraft had a mass of 36806 kg.\n\n## Causes and Aftermath\n\nThe severity of the pogo oscillations on Apollo 6 was surprising because a test article flown on Apollo 6 had the shape and weight of a real lander in the adapter, and this change in mass distribution coupled back into the fuel system problem and increased the pogo oscillations. NASA formed a Pogo Working Group comprised of more than 1,000 government and industry engineers, and determined the key to mitigating the pogo effect included 'de-tuning' the rocket's engine to change the frequency of the vibration it produced by filling the prevalve cavities on the liquid oxygen (LOX) feed lines with helium gas.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8a688fcf-31f8-4434-9908-40a9ad136b7e","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:37.689Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6f9c6eaf-94e1-4213-8b60-0a6507919e75","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Additionally, weighing 95,231 pounds, Apollo 9 was the heaviest crewed spacecraft.\n\n## Mission Duration\nThe goal of their 10-day Earth orbital mission was to test the Lunar Module (LM), the vehicle designed to land astronauts on the Moon's surface.\n\n## Primary Objectives\nThe mission was flown to qualify the LM for lunar orbit operations in preparation for the first Moon landing by demonstrating its descent and ascent propulsion systems, showing that its crew could fly it independently, then rendezvous and dock with the CSM again, as would be required for the first crewed lunar landing.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8759f73e-c9f9-4f64-9f66-ac384c674737","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:51.408Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"be17078d-de2e-4472-b196-b75ea4149d93","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It was designated an \"F\" mission, intended to test all spacecraft components and procedures short of actual descent and landing.\n\n## Crew\nThe prime crew consisted of astronauts Thomas P. Stafford, commander; John W. Young, command module pilot; and Eugene A. Cernan, lunar module pilot.\n\n## Launch and Duration\nThe veteran crew lifted off Launch Pad 39B at NASA's Kennedy Space Center in Florida on May 18, 1969. Splashdown occurred in the Pacific Ocean on May 26, 1969, making the mission approximately 8 days in duration.\n\n## Spacecraft Mass\nThe total mass was 28834 kg.\n\n## Lunar Orbit Details\nApollo 10 entered an initial 315.5 km x 110.4 km lunar orbit on 21 May 1969, and a second burn circularized the orbit to 113.9 km x 109.1 km.\n\n## Closest Approach\nAstronauts Thomas Stafford and Gene Cernan flew the Apollo Lunar Module to within 14.4 kilometers (7.8 nautical miles; 9 miles) of the lunar surface. Docking was complete after 8 hours 10 minutes 5 seconds of lunar flight.\n\n## Return\nAfter four orbits they rejoined Young in the CSM and, after the CSM completed its 31st orbit of the Moon, they returned safely to Earth.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8a692278-dcb9-4628-b840-9a3b236b53ae","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:52.601Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4ff3ef41-6a19-4d2e-8eff-a0e4d3e5da05","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

In making a pinpoint landing, they showed that NASA could plan future missions in the expectation that astronauts could land close to sites of scientific interest.\n\n## Crew\n\nAboard the LM were astronauts Charles Conrad Jr., commander; and Alan L. Bean, lunar module pilot. Astronaut Richard R. Gordon Jr., command module pilot, remained with the CSM in lunar orbit while Conrad and Bean descended in the LM to explore the surface of the moon.\n\n## EVA Duration\n\nIn their first lunar exploration, Conrad spent three hours, 39 minutes outside Intrepid, and Bean logged two hours, 58 minutes on the lunar terrain. The second EVA lasted three hours, 48 minutes.\n\n## Samples Collected\n\nThe second EVA included the collection of 70 pounds of rock and dirt samples, the retrieval of 10 to 15 pounds of randomly selected selenological samples.\n\n## ALSEP Deployment\n\nDeployment of the ALSEP took about an hour to complete. The central station, located at 3.0094 S latitude, 23.4246 W longitude, was turned on at 14:21 UT on 19 November 1969. The Apollo 12 ALSEP instruments consisted of: (1) a passive seismometer, designed to measure seismic activity and physical properties of the lunar crust and interior; (2) a suprathermal ion detector, designed to measure the flux, composition, energy, and velocity of low-energy positive ions; (3) a cold cathode ion gauge, designed to measure the atmosphere and any variations with time or solar activity such atmosphere may have; (4) a lunar dust detector, to measure dust accumulation, radiation damage to solar cells, and reflected infrared energy and temperatures; (5) a lunar surface magnetometer (LSM), designed to measure the magnetic field at the lunar surface; and (6) a solar wind spectrometer, which measured the fluxes and spectra of the electrons and protons that emanate from the Sun and reach the lunar surface.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b3ac3fbd-6add-40de-86a3-caadcb8ef351","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:55.362Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"301a7e15-66a0-4900-810e-b132e279fd3d","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Part 3 (1971)\",\"url\":\"https://history.nasa.gov/SP-4009/v4p3h.htm\"}]\n\nBased on the web search results, here are the key details about Apollo 14's Fra Mauro landing:\n\n## Mission Crew and Landing\nThe Apollo 14 crew consisted of Alan B. Shepard, Edgar D. Mitchell, and Stuart A. Roosa, with Shepard and Mitchell landing on the Moon on February 5, 1971. The landing point was in the hilly uplands of the Fra Mauro crater, approximately 110 miles east of the Apollo 12 landing site.\n\n## EVA Activities\nThe first EVA period included ALSEP deployment and lasted four hours, 49 minutes. The second EVA lasted four hours, 35 minutes, resulting in a total EVA time of nine hours, 24 minutes. Shepard set a new distance-traveled record on the lunar surface of approximately 9,000 feet.\n\n## Samples and ALSEP\nA total of 94 pounds (43 kg) of Moon rocks were brought back from Apollo 14. The surface experiments included the Apollo Lunar Surface Experiment Package (ALSEP) and several independent investigations. It was the first J mission, with a longer stay on the Moon and a greater focus on science than earlier landings.\n\n## Lunar Roving Vehicle\nApollo 15 saw the first use of the Lunar Roving Vehicle. On July 31, 1971, the Lunar Roving Vehicle (LRV) made its lunar debut. The LRV, developed by Marshall Space Flight Center in Huntsville, Alabama, would travel just over 17 miles, logging three hours and two minutes of driving time.\n\n## Crew\nAboard the Apollo 15 spacecraft are astronauts David R. Scott, commander, Alfred M. Worden, command module pilot, and James B. Irwin, lunar module pilot. Commander David Scott and Lunar Module Pilot James Irwin landed near Hadley Rille and explored the local area using the rover, allowing them to travel further from the Lunar Module than had been possible on previous missions.\n\n## EVA Activities and Samples\nThey spent 18½ hours on the Moon's surface on four extravehicular activities (EVA), and collected 170 pounds (77 kg) of surface material. After concluding their exploration stops, they returned to the lander to drop off their samples and prepare to set up the Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package (ALSEP), the scientific instruments that would remain when they left.\n\n## Landing Site\nThe landing site was at the Hadley-Apennine location.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"655d4a82-9b67-47dc-a649-e6ee04f9baa6","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:09.724Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4648838b-da44-4b2c-81fb-b0d7e5cf3721","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/missions/apollo/apollo-13-the-successful-failure/\"},{\"title\":\"Apollo 13\",\"url\":\"https://sma.nasa.gov/sma-disciplines/accllp/apollo-13\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about the Apollo 13 explosion and oxygen tank failure:\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nApollo 13 (April 11–17, 1970) was the seventh crewed mission in the Apollo space program and would have been the third Moon landing. The craft was launched from Kennedy Space Center on April 11, 1970, but the landing was aborted after an oxygen tank in the service module (SM) exploded two days into the mission, disabling its electrical and life-support system. The mission was commanded by Jim Lovell, with Jack Swigert as command module (CM) pilot and Fred Haise as Lunar Module (LM) pilot.\n\n## The Explosion and Duration\n\nThe accident occurred at approximately 55 hours and 55 minutes into the flight, with the crew reporting an electrical event accompanied by a loud bang, followed a few minutes later by a visual sighting of the spacecraft venting something overboard. The sudden failure of Oxygen Tank 2 compromised Oxygen Tank 1, causing its contents to leak out, possibly through a damaged line or valve, over the next 130 minutes, entirely depleting the SM's oxygen supply.\n\n## Root Cause of the Oxygen Tank Failure\n\nA routine stir of an oxygen tank ignited damaged wire insulation inside it, causing an explosion that vented the contents of both of the SM's oxygen tanks to space. The underlying design flaw was critical: In 1965, the CM had undergone many improvements that included raising the permissible voltage to the heaters in the oxygen tanks from 28 to 65 volts DC. Unfortunately, the thermostatic switches on these heaters weren't modified to suit the change. This subjected the wiring in the vicinity of the heaters to very high temperatures (1000°F), which have been subsequently shown to severely degrade teflon insulation. The thermostatic switches started to open while powered by 65 volts DC and were probably welded shut.\n\n## Successful Failure\n\nThe crew, supported by backup systems on the Apollo Lunar Module, instead looped around the Moon in a circumlunar trajectory and returned safely to Earth on April 17. Still, it was classified as a \"successful failure\" because of the experience gained in rescuing the crew.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"03515c6f-31a7-4a1b-9a6e-77f942227a42","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:11.323Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c2876952-b758-4758-b1e3-a51c142d6b1b","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The landing and exploration were in the Descartes Highlands, a site chosen because some scientists expected it to be an area formed by volcanic action, though this proved not to be the case.\n\n## Crew\nThe mission was crewed by Commander John Young, Lunar Module Pilot Charles Duke and Command Module Pilot Ken Mattingly.\n\n## Landing Details\nThe lunar module carrying John Young and Charles Duke touched down at Descartes about 276 meters northwest of planned point (8 degrees 59′ 29″S, 15 degrees 30′ 52″E) at about 9:24 p.m. EST April 20, about five hours, 43 minutes late.\n\n## Surface Operations and EVA Activities\nDuring 71 hours, two minutes surface stay, astronauts explored region on three EVAs totaling 20 hours, 14 minutes. More specifically, the problem was traced to a failed integrated circuit in the pitch guidance module. The main Centaur engine was ignited 265 seconds after launch, but the upper stage began to oscillate in pitch and tumbled out of control. The Centaur stage shut down 365 seconds after launch due to starvation caused by the tumbling.\n\n## Final Outcome\nThe Centaur and spacecraft payload separated and re-entered the Earth's atmosphere approximately 1,500 kilometres (930 mi) downrange and fell to the Atlantic Ocean about 560 kilometres (350 mi) north of Puerto Rico.\n\n## Impact on Mars Exploration\nMariner 9, the second of the pair of spacecraft created under NASA's Mariner Mars 71 project, successfully took on Mariner 8's objective to map 70 percent of the Martian surface.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0c601ae1-3830-4834-acb0-26de55500912","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:25.095Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"757220ed-51fe-4bf0-b833-203be43eb23a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Initial orbital parameters were about 870 × 11,130 miles (1,398 × 17,916 kilometers) at a 64.3-degree inclination. More specifically, the landing occurred on Nov. 9, 1967, at 3:37:08 p.m. EST (20:37:08 UT), which confirms the flight duration was approximately 8 hours and 37 minutes.\n\n## Instruments and Payload\n\nThe mission was designed to test all aspects of the Saturn V launch vehicle and also returned pictures of Earth taken by the automatic Command Module apogee camera from about one hour before to one hour after apogee.\n\n## Mission Objectives\n\nMission objectives included testing of structural integrity, compatibility of launch vehicle and spacecraft, heat shield and thermal seal integrity, overall reentry operations, launch loads and dynamic characteristics, stage separation, launch vehicle subsystems, the emergency detection system, and mission support facilities and operations.\n\n## Orbit Parameters\n\nOrbital insertion was achieved by ignition of the third (S-IVB) stage, putting the spacecraft (S-IVB and CSM) into a 184 x 192 km parking orbit with a period of 88.2 minutes and an inclination of 32.6 degrees. After two orbits the S-IVB was re-ignited for a simulated translunar injection burn, putting the spacecraft into an Earth-intersecting trajectory with an apogee of 17,346 km.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"e9d77c55-6fbc-43c3-9960-ee09a4137d7a","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:26.043Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"20976463-9f31-46ce-ad20-d634359d18ae","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

An additional second-stage engine also shut down early due to cross-wiring with the engine that had shut down. The damaged third-stage engine failed to restart for trans-lunar injection.\n\nThe vibrations that caused the engine damage were due to pogo oscillations in the Saturn V's S-IC first stage, which caused the vehicle to experience a g-force of ±0.6 g, though it had only been designed for a maximum of 0.25 g.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"555fec81-03b9-4927-9311-2996e94f431b","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:27.154Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5f713ac7-6607-4346-93cd-53e0a40de81f","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Command Module Pilot Alfred Worden orbited the Moon, operating the sensors in the scientific instrument module (SIM) bay of the service module.\n\n## Instruments and Their Functions\n\nThe suite of instruments collected data on the Moon and its environment using a panoramic camera, a gamma-ray spectrometer, a mapping camera, a laser altimeter, a mass spectrometer, and a lunar subsatellite deployed at the end of the moonwalks.\n\nThe **mass spectrometer** specifically: The lunar orbital science experiments package included a mass spectrometer experiment whose objective was to use the measured composition data to study the sources, sinks, and transport mechanisms of the ambient lunar atmosphere. The analyzer was mounted on a retractable boom. When fully extended, the boom placed the spectrometer 7.3 m from the spacecraft, a distance anticipated to be beyond the outgassed molecular cloud.\n\n## Subsatellite\n\nThe Apollo 15 Particles and Fields Subsatellite (PFS-1) was a small satellite released into lunar orbit from the SIM bay just before the mission left orbit to return to Earth. Its main objectives were to study the plasma, particle, and magnetic field environment of the Moon and map the lunar gravity field.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d0c45d93-e5a5-4bfa-9c73-db86e4b42164","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:42.268Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"4863f7ec-7af5-4d34-b1c6-e73c5d9f551f","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It was a portable device to determine the Moon's internal structure by taking measurements at the immediate landing site and at remote locations during the traverses.\n\n## Cosmic Ray Detector\n\nThe Lunar Surface Cosmic Ray Experiment (LSCRE) consisted of two particle detectors deployed for about two days on the Moon, one exposed to the Sun, the other kept in the shade, to measure the flux of cosmic rays.\n\n## Lunar Surface Gravimeter\n\nThe LSG's major goal was to confirm the existence of gravity waves as predicted by Einstein, measure the response of the Moon to the Earth's tidal pull, and detect free lunar oscillations in periods of 15 minutes or longer. It could also measure vertical components of seismic events with frequencies up to 16 cycles per second.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3b8fd2b4-f061-49fc-8093-806cab88156a","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:44.412Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c4c40d12-58d0-4265-b127-af33e65311f6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

A second strike at 52 seconds knocked out the \"8-ball\" attitude indicator. The static discharge caused a voltage transient that knocked all three fuel cells offline, meaning the spacecraft was being powered entirely from its batteries, which could not supply enough current to meet demand.\n\n## CSM Mass\n\nThe search results did not contain specific mass or weight information in kilograms for the Apollo 12 CSM. Schmitt was assigned in August 1971 to fly on Apollo 17, replacing Joe Engle as Lunar Module Pilot, and landed on the Moon with commander Gene Cernan in December 1972. As a geologist, Schmitt was the first true scientist to explore the Moon.\n\n## The Orange Soil Discovery\n\nOrange soil was discovered at Shorty crater; it proved to be volcanic in origin, although from early in the Moon's history. The orange soil is believed to be small bits of rapidly-cooled molten rock ejected in a lava fountain. Geologist Schmitt discovered orange lunar soil near the spot at Shorty Crater.\n\n## Mission Details\n\nThe Apollo 17 crew returned with 110 kilograms of rock and soil samples, more than was returned from any of the other lunar landing sites.\n\nNote: The search results did not contain specific information about the CSM mass of 30320 kg or famous quotes from Harrison Schmitt. Vol. IV. Appendix 9\",\"url\":\"https://www.hq.nasa.gov/office/pao/History/SP-4009/v4app9.htm\"},{\"title\":\"ALSEP Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/history/alsj/HamishALSEP.html\"},{\"title\":\"Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package - Wikipedia\",\"url\":\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiments_Package\"},{\"title\":\"Far-Ultraviolet Camera/Spectroscope - the NSSDCA\",\"url\":\"https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/experiment/display.action?id=1972-031C-10\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here's comprehensive information about Apollo 16's far UV camera and related instruments:\n\n## Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph\n\nThe Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph (UVC) was one of the experiments deployed on the lunar surface by the Apollo 16 astronauts, consisting of a telescope and camera that obtained astronomical images and spectra in the far ultraviolet region of the electromagnetic spectrum.\n\n**Technical Specifications:**\nThe Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph was a tripod mounted, f/1.0, 75 mm electronographic Schmidt camera weighing 22 kg. Spectroscopic data were provided from 300 to 1350 Ångström, with 30 Å resolution, and images were provided in two passbands ranges, 1050–1260 Å and 1200–1550 Å. The camera contained a cesium iodide (CsI) photocathode and used a film cartridge which was recovered and returned to earth for processing.\n\n**Deployment and Location:**\nApollo 16 astronauts placed the observatory on the Moon in April 1972, where it sits today on the Moon's Descartes highland region, in the shadow of the lunar module Orion. The astronauts deployed the camera in the shadow of the LM and then pointed it toward objects of interest, including the geocorona, the earth's atmosphere, the solar wind, various nebulae, the Milky Way, galactic clusters and other galactic objects, intergalactic hydrogen, solar bow cloud, the lunar atmosphere, and lunar volcanic gases.\n\n**Principal Investigator:**\nThe principal investigator and chief engineer of the Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph was Dr. George Robert Carruthers, who was working at the US Naval Research Lab.\n\n## Apollo 16 ALSEP and Other Experiments\n\nThe LM also carried a Lunar Roving Vehicle (LRV), an Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package (ALSEP) that contained scientific experiments to be deployed and left on the lunar surface, and other scientific and sample collection apparatus.\n\n**Orbital Science:**\nOrbital science experiments were concentrated in an array of instruments and cameras in the scientific instrument module, or SIM, bay. Thomas Mattingly orbited the moon with cameras and SIM bay instruments operating during the surface stay of Young and Duke, and the results verified Apollo 15 data and provided information on lunar terrain not previously covered.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0292fe0b-9ccb-42b7-92c1-5fa47f4975c6","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:02.998Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"04904d26-bcff-4ef8-ac8d-7f77c9662d76","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Let me provide you with what I discovered:\n\nThe Apollo Lunar Surface Experiments Package (ALSEP) comprised a set of scientific instruments placed by the astronauts at the landing site of each of the five Apollo missions to land on the Moon following Apollo 11 (Apollos 12, 14, 15, 16, and 17). Apollo 11 left a smaller package called the Early Apollo Scientific Experiments Package, or EASEP.\n\nEach mission had a different array of experiments.\n\nFor Apollo 17 specifically, the ALSEP experiments were a Heat Flow Experiment (HFE), to measure the rate of heat flow from the interior of the Moon, a Lunar Surface Gravimeter (LSG), to measure alterations in the lunar gravity field at the site, a Lunar Atmospheric Composition Experiment (LACE), to investigate what the lunar atmosphere is made up of, a Lunar Seismic Profiling Experiment (LSPE), to detect nearby seismic activity, and a Lunar Ejecta and Meteorites Experiment (LEME), to measure the velocity and energy of dust particles.\n\nThe search results indicate that experiments varied by mission, but don't provide complete mission-by-mission breakdowns for Apollo 11, 12, 14, 15, and 16. The Wikipedia article and NASA sources reference additional experiments but the specific combinations for each mission aren't fully detailed in these results. Here's a summary of the key findings:\n\n## Apollo 11 EASEP Instruments\n\nApollo 11 left a smaller package called the Early Apollo Scientific Experiments Package, or EASEP.\n\n### Main Instruments:\n\n**Passive Seismic Experiment (PSEP):** The Passive Seismic Experiment Package was a scientific experiment deployed on the lunar surface by the astronauts of Apollo 11 as part of the Early Apollo Surface Experiments Package. The experiment's goal was to determine the structure, tectonic activity, physical nature, and composition of the Moon. The experiment's sensor system was made of two parts: three low-frequency seismometers in an orthogonal triaxial arrangement to capture horizontal and vertical movement, and one single-axis high-frequency seismometer that only measured vertical motion.\n\n**Laser Ranging Retroreflector (LRRR):** The laser ranging retroreflector was a retroreflector array with a folding support structure for aiming and aligning the array toward earth. The array was built of cubes of fused silica. Laser ranging beams from earth were reflected back to their point of origin for precise measurement of earth-moon distance, motion of the moon's center of mass, lunar radius, and earth geophysical information.\n\n**Solar Wind Composition Experiment:** NASA added a Solar Wind Composition Experiment, consisting of an aluminum foil panel to collect particles emitted by the Sun to determine their elemental composition.\n\n**Other Components:** The EASEP also consisted of two solar panels to provide power, an antenna and communications system to send data to Earth ground stations and receive commands, and a lunar dust detector to measure dust accumulation and radiation damage to solar cells.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"581f5c78-046c-4f5d-ad0f-bfd1f7a07b6e","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:54.176Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7499a0bc-2ee4-4ab1-96e0-fb71337bd433","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Irwin (LMP)\n- **cost_millions_usd:** 445 (average)\n- **instruments:** ALSEP: passive seismometer, lunar surface magnetometer, solar wind spectrometer, suprathermal ion detector, cold cathode ion gauge, heat flow experiment, lunar dust detector. SIM bay: panoramic camera, gamma-ray spectrometer, X-ray spectrometer, mapping camera, laser altimeter, mass spectrometer, alpha-particle spectrometer. Particles and Fields Subsatellite (PFS-1) deployed\n- **orbit_params:** Lunar orbit: ~109 x 316 km initial; circularized to ~100 km\n- **landing_coords:** 26.1322 N, 3.6339 E (Hadley-Apennine, at foot of Apennine Mountains near Hadley Rille)\n- **eva_hours:** 18.5 (Stand-up EVA: 0.55h; EVA1: 6h 33m; EVA2: 7h 12m; EVA3: 4h 50m)\n- **samples_kg:** 77.31 (170 lbs)\n- **key_results:** First J-mission (extended lunar stay). First Lunar Roving Vehicle (LRV): traveled 27.9 km in 3h 2m of driving. Found the \"Genesis Rock\" (sample 15415, anorthosite ~4.1 billion years old). First SIM bay orbital science experiments. First lunar subsatellite. First deep-space EVA (Worden, during return). 3 days on surface (66h 55m). First stand-up EVA from LM hatch. Worden performed 38-min EVA to retrieve SIM bay film cassettes during return\n- **failure_details:** One parachute failed during splashdown (2 of 3 functioned normally, safe landing). Postflight: crew reprimanded for carrying unauthorized postal covers to Moon\n- **firsts:** First LRV; first J-mission; first SIM bay; first lunar subsatellite; first stand-up EVA; first deep-space EVA; first lunar orbit science experiments\n- **famous_quotes:** \"Man must explore. And this is exploration at its greatest.\" (Scott, upon stepping onto Moon); \"I think we found what we came for.\" (Scott, upon discovering Genesis Rock)\n\n---\n\n**1.81 | Pioneer 10**\n- **launch_vehicle:** Atlas-Centaur with TE-M-364-4 third stage (Star-37E solid motor)\n- **launch_site:** LC-36A, Cape Canaveral\n- **launch_date_utc:** 1972-03-03T01:49:00Z\n- **end_date:** 2003-01-23 (last signal); science mission ended 1997-03-31\n- **duration_hours:** ~270,720 (30+ years operational; 21-month primary mission)\n- **mass_kg:** 258\n- **crew:** Uncrewed (robotic)\n- **cost_millions_usd:** ~350 (total mission through 1997, FY2001 dollars); $380M combined contract for Pioneer 10+11\n- **instruments:** 11 scientific instruments: helium vector magnetometer, quadrispherical plasma analyzer, charged particle instrument, cosmic ray telescope, Geiger tube telescope, trapped radiation detector, meteoroid detectors, asteroid/meteoroid detector, ultraviolet photometer, imaging photopolarimeter, infrared radiometer\n- **orbit_params:** Heliocentric escape trajectory; Jupiter closest approach: 130,354 km at 02:26 UTC Dec 4, 1973\n- **landing_coords:** N/A\n- **eva_hours:** 0\n- **samples_kg:** 0\n- **key_results:** First spacecraft to traverse asteroid belt (July 1972 - Feb 1973). First Jupiter flyby (Dec 4, 1973). First spacecraft to achieve solar system escape velocity. Returned first close-up images of Jupiter. Discovered Jupiter's radiation belts far more intense than predicted. Last signal received Jan 23, 2003 at 12 billion km (80 AU). Carries gold-anodized aluminum plaque depicting humans and Earth's location\n- **failure_details:** None during primary mission. Pioneer anomaly: unexplained slight deceleration (later attributed to thermal radiation pressure)\n- **firsts:** First spacecraft through asteroid belt; first Jupiter flyby; first solar system escape; first spacecraft to pass beyond all known planets\n- **famous_quotes:** N/A (but the Pioneer Plaque became iconic)\n\n---\n\n**1.82 | Apollo 16 (AS-511)**\n- **launch_vehicle:** Saturn V (SA-511)\n- **launch_site:** LC-39A, Kennedy Space Center\n- **launch_date_utc:** 1972-04-16T17:54:00Z\n- **end_date:** 1972-04-27\n- **duration_hours:** 265.9 (11d 1h 51m)\n- **mass_kg:** 30,354 (CSM-113 Casper)\n- **crew:** John W. Young (CDR), Thomas K. \"Ken\" Mattingly II (CMP), Charles M. Duke Jr. (LMP)\n- **cost_millions_usd:** 445 (average)\n- **instruments:** ALSEP: passive seismometer, active seismic experiment, heat flow experiment (failed), lunar surface magnetometer, Far Ultraviolet Camera/Spectrograph (first astronomical observatory on Moon). SIM bay: panoramic/mapping cameras, gamma-ray/X-ray/alpha-particle spectrometers, mass spectrometer, laser altimeter. Particles and Fields Subsatellite (PFS-2). Cosmic Ray Detector, Portable Magnetometer\n- **orbit_params:** Lunar orbit: ~108 x 316 km initial; circularized to ~100 km\n- **landing_coords:** 8.9730 S, 15.5002 E (Descartes Highlands)\n- **eva_hours:** 20.23 (EVA1: 7h 11m; EVA2: 7h 23m; EVA3: 5h 40m)\n- **samples_kg:** 95.71 (211 lbs)\n- **key_results:** First exploration of lunar highlands. Disproved volcanic origin theory for Descartes region (found impact breccias, not volcanic rocks). LRV traveled 26.7 km total. Surface stay: 71h 2m. Far UV Camera/Spectrograph was first astronomical observatory on another world. Duke (age 36) was youngest moonwalker. Mattingly performed 1h 24m deep-space EVA. Duke left family photo on lunar surface\n- **failure_details:** 6-hour delay in lunar orbit due to CSM SPS engine oscillation concern (resolved). Heat flow experiment cables snagged and severed by Young during EVA. Lunar subsatellite PFS-2 crashed into Moon after 35 days (unfavorable orbit)\n- **firsts:** First lunar highlands landing; first astronomical observatory on another world (Far UV Camera); youngest moonwalker (Duke, 36)\n- **famous_quotes:** \"There you are: Mysterious and unknown Descartes. Highland plains. Apollo 16 is gonna change your image.\" (Young)\n\n---\n\n**1.83 | Apollo 17 (AS-512)**\n- **launch_vehicle:** Saturn V (SA-512)\n- **launch_site:** LC-39A, Kennedy Space Center\n- **launch_date_utc:** 1972-12-07T05:33:00Z\n- **end_date:** 1972-12-19\n- **duration_hours:** 301.9 (12d 13h 52m)\n- **mass_kg:** 30,320 (CSM-114 America; CM: 5,960, SM: 24,360); LM-12 Challenger: 16,456\n- **crew:** Eugene A. Cernan (CDR), Ronald E. Evans (CMP), Harrison H. \"Jack\" Schmitt (LMP)\n- **cost_millions_usd:** 445 (average)\n- **instruments:** ALSEP: heat flow experiment (HFE), lunar seismic profiling experiment (LSPE), lunar atmospheric composition experiment (LACE), lunar ejecta and meteorites experiment (LEAM), lunar surface gravimeter (LSG). Additional surface: traverse gravimeter experiment (TGE), cosmic ray detector (CRD), neutron probe, surface electrical properties experiment (SEP). SIM bay: panoramic/mapping cameras, IR radiometer, UV spectrometer, lunar sounder\n- **orbit_params:** Lunar orbit: ~109 x 316 km initial; circularized to ~109 km\n- **landing_coords:** 20.1911 N, 30.7723 E (Taurus-Littrow valley)\n- **eva_hours:** 22.07 (EVA1: 7h 12m; EVA2: 7h 37m; EVA3: 7h 15m)\n- **samples_kg:** 110.4 (243 lbs -- most of any mission)\n- **key_results:** Final Apollo Moon landing. Longest lunar surface stay: 75 hours. Most EVA time: 22h 4m. Most samples returned: 110.4 kg. Longest total mission duration. LRV traversed 30.5 km. Schmitt discovered orange volcanic soil at Shorty Crater (4 billion year old volcanic glass beads). First professional scientist (geologist) on Moon. First nighttime launch of Saturn V. Evans performed 1h 6m deep-space EVA. Set multiple records: longest Moon stay, most EVAs, most samples, longest total mission\n- **failure_details:** 2h 40m launch delay due to launch sequencer malfunction. Fender on LRV broke (repaired with duct tape and geological maps). Otherwise fully successful\n- **firsts:** First scientist (geologist) on Moon; first nighttime Saturn V launch; longest lunar surface stay (75h); most samples returned (110.4 kg); most total EVA time (22h 4m); last humans on Moon (to date)\n- **famous_quotes:** \"As we leave the Moon at Taurus-Littrow, we leave as we came, and, God willing, as we shall return, with peace and hope for all mankind.\" (Cernan, last words spoken on lunar surface); \"There is orange soil!!\" (Schmitt, discovering volcanic glass at Shorty Crater)\n\n---\n\n## Summary Statistics\n\n| Mission | Duration (h) |
